		<date>2014-11-28</date>
		<username>Adam Foidart</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>In this mirror world, we don't need no rules! Unless we do.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Mirrors" is an attempt at an American remake of a Korean horror film and it just doesn't translate. Even if you're not familiar with the original story, there are constant inconsistencies present and a climax that shatters the film like a tea cup thrown out onto the pavement. The premise is that an ex-police officer (Kiefer Sutherland as Ben) that is down on his luck is hired as a night guard at an abandoned shopping center. There he begins seeing strange images inside the mirrors of the building. They turn out to be far more than just hallucinations; they're deadly. If he wants these horrifying images to stop and his family to be safe he will have to solve a good ol' fashioned horror-ghost mystery.If you're a fan of what I call "J-Horror" films, you will see an overall story that's pretty familiar. You expect the movie to set up some terrifying visions, a quest for the hero, a couple of deaths here and there and a terrifying mystery in the end. The trick will be to get to the bottom of the catastrophe while avoiding any mirror surfaces, which are conduits for the ghostly forces. Trouble begins when The rules set up early in the movie keep getting broken. The further you get in, the more it becomes both confusing and frustrating. For example, the main way that mirrors kill people in the film is by having the reflections act on their own and force people to commit suicide (it's odd, but that's the general idea). Towards the climax of the film this changes completely. Sometimes reflections attack other people or appear without the person who created them being in the room. The reflections feel more like evil ghost duplicates than actual evil versions of themselves that are trapped in reflective surfaces. There are even some scenes where people are being attacked without any reflection present, so in the end you just give up trying to understand what's going on with the movie. Another inconsistency is in the sounds. Most of the time, the mirrors don't make any noise. That makes sense because they're just images reflected that gain the supernatural ability to influence people instead of the other way around. Once again towards the end of the film you hear some reflections making noises and talking and it doesn't make any sense. In theory it would work because just when you think your character is safe, suddenly he or she will be in mortal peril, but in practice it just feels like the script was quickly written with additional scares thrown in without anyone taking a second look at them to see if they worked with the film's pre-established rules.Another big flaw in the film is that it isn't very frightening. You'll be jumping out of your seat plenty, but that's because the movie uses cheap tactics to startle you. If it isn't a quick flash of a grotesque mutilated body accompanied by a music sting, it's a sudden flapping of bird wings or a door slamming. The movie has some genuinely creepy looking sets and some unsettling scenes but instead of building suspense and letting the chills slowly come to you as you start playing with possible scenarios in your head, it just interrupts itself with these cheap sound cues and loud noises. It's a shame because the movie actually starts off pretty creepy. You have this really big derelict shopping center that was the setting for a big tragedy and our main character is stuck there for hours by himself in the dark. There are creepy burnt manikins, perfect for setting up an unsuspecting sneak onto Ben... but it just drops that entirely by making the bulk of the film set in a series of different location. It doesn't help that the characters in the movie make the same mistakes as every other character that sees ghosts and is forced to solve a supernatural mystery. Ben acts erratically, breaking mirrors and painting over them without giving any explanations to the people around him and he doesn't ever try to collect evidence that there are weird things going on. He never tries to convince the people he is trying to protect that he isn't crazy, just so we can have the scene where he gets into a fight with the people that don't believe him.Then, we get to that ending. This is A MINOR SPOILER, but we're basically told that long ago the problem of the ghosts/mirrors/demonic creatures was tackled by exorcists and priests and that they had no success in taming this threat. This information is not only inconsistent with the end of the movie, but it makes the behaviour of the characters in the film totally unexplainable. The ridiculous reason for the mirrors being dangerous is nothing compared to the climax of the film, which is taken from a totally different genre and is downright silly. It would be wrong to spoil exactly what happens in the scenes right before the very end of the movie but if you see this confrontation you'll be shaking your head in embarrassment.The movie isn't a complete disaster but there are points where you can totally tell that the original story just wouldn't translate into a North American setting and nobody knew what to do. Solution? Pick something at random from another horror movie and fill in the gaps. It ends up playing like a terrible Asian ghost movie and a terrible American haunted house story mashed together and coarsely stitched so it would stay in place just long enough for the film to conclude. If the concept of "Mirrors" sounds interesting to you, track down the original source material, or just grab a good Asian horror film. Don't bother sitting through this 111 minute train wreck because you'll be really frustrated as everything that looked promising at first falls flat on its face. (Dvd, April 11, 2013)</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>My reflections on Mirrors?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Director Alexandre Aja's Mirrors boasts a decent cast and stunning gore courtesy of FX experts Howard Berger and Greg Nicotero, but misses being a completely satisfying horror experience by failing to adhere to a convincing or consistent internal logic.At first, the mirrors are the gateway by which the evil force finds its way into our world; later, it can use any reflective surface?which is just as dumb as it sounds (almost any smooth surface has a reflection; it would be impossible to avoid them all!). The evil attacks the night-watchmen working at the department store, but doesn't bother with the old guard who patrols the building during the day. Most ridiculous is Ben Carson's continuing to work at the store, even after experiencing several supernatural incidents that would send most people screaming, never to return. These kind of problems plague the film to the end, seriously undermining its effectiveness.Still, at least the film doesn't wimp out on the gore, opening with a splattery throat slashing, giving us a yucky autopsy scene, and delivering a thoroughly shocking sequence in which Amy Smart suffers a grisly bathtub demise, her jaw ripped clean off her face (the same scene also provides the obligatory female nudity, Smart kindly baring boobs and butt before getting in the bath). Aja also sees fit to include that other popular mainstay of the genre: a fit bird in a tight white vest, Paula Patton as Carson's wife doing the honours, even going so far as to get all wet for the film's finale.5.5 out of 10, but I refuse to round it up to a 6 on account of that awful special effects scene in which Sutherland writhes unconvincingly in pain on the floor as he is burned by truly terrible CGI fire.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Scary And Confusing At The Same Time</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I love a good ghost or haunted house movie, and that is basically what "Mirrors" is all about; Kiefer Sutherland plays a former cop who takes a job as a security guard at a haunted department store, but since every decent haunted house movie needs a plot device, this one focuses on mirrors and reflections, and doesn't let it go. It starts very atmospheric and spooky, and then it accelerates into a far-fetched mind bender with a lot of exposition. Maybe a bit too fast? I've seen a lot of horror movies, both good and bad, and it seems the leap of logic Kiefer makes in the movie from "This place is haunted." to "It's something about the mirrors." was kind of a huge step for his character. Rational people do experience paranormal activity without blaming it on random objects. It's kind of like Jack Nicholson in the middle of "The Shining" blaming the chimney or Geoffrey Rush in the middle of "House On Haunted Hill" suddenly blaming a moose head on a wall. I also think it was a bit of a huge coincidence that Kiefer's character just happens to also be formerly married to the city coroner in order to get the access he needs to do his investigation on the burned out store, and even then, I also don't see why he tries connecting the location to one man's grisly death scene. I mean, I don't get why Kiefer thinks seeing the man's body or cause of death will give him details on the haunting except to set him up for another mirror reflection illusion. He could have got everything he needed to know in one phone call. Why not just do a straight haunted house movie? There is just so much potential in a haunted and abandoned department store that anything else would be exposition. Another thing I didn't care for is that the movie is the unnecessary gore. It kind of stands out in the movie. Amy Smart plays Kiefer's sister and rips her jaw off in the bathtub (why do hot actresses always meet their end in the bathroom?), and the cops don't blink twice. Do you know how strong someone would have to be to do that, and yet, Kiefer's character immediately becomes suspect? They don't bother speculating about a ten-foot guy on steroids? Bottom line, simplified haunted house movies usually work better, but the trickery and reflection motifs in the movie done with mirrors and illusion is very impressive.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Learn to do your hair without a mirror before you watch this...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There are basically two ways to Judge a horror movie. The first is to look purely at the quality of the production, things like acting, lighting and writing etc. The second is to simply ask yourself 'did it scare me?' Mirrors is one of those few movies that scores high on both accounts, not only is it well put together as a movie, it's also going to raise the hairs on the back of your neck and have you clutching your armrest. Simply put, Mirrors is a demon/ghost story where the evil spirit makes use of mirrors to interact with humans. Thru the mirrors this unholy reflection causes all kinds of nastiness, including one very gory, yet refreshingly original, death in a bathtub. However, the film has enough of a murder mystery element to keep you guessing at the real nature of the threat until the very end, so don't think this is just another typical ghost story. Director and writer Alexandre Aja has put some real effort into making a film that captivates audiences, not only with high quality special effects and unexpected scare moments but also, and perhaps more impressively, with an engaging storyline and likable characters. Which brings me to the acting. Having always been very indifferent to Kiefer Sutherland I was surprised to find myself really rooting for the guy as he tries desperately to save his family and not lose what little sanity he has left in the process. His estranged wife is caught between trying to help her beloved husband get a grip on reality and protecting her children from daddy who has been unstable at best and has now taken to painting, punching and shooting mirrors. The acting on everyone's part is convincing, including young Cameron Boyce who can go from cute and adorable six-year old to evil demon spawn in seconds. All in all Mirrors is good for several good scares and has a better storyline than you might expect from a horror movie. I recommend this movie.</fullReview>

		<score>0.1</score>
		<shortDescription>Do Yourself A Favor ? Watch "Tetsuo" (1989) or "Rubber's Lover" (1996) Instead</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Japanese remake cyberpunk horror is a hit-or-miss sub-genre. On the one hand we have highly entertaining movies like "Tetsuo: The Iron Man" (1989) and "Rubber's Lover" (1996), while on the other hand we have feats of mediocrity like "Hellevator" (2004) and "Meatball Machine" (2005) or poorly made crapfests like "A Tale of Two Sisters" (2006). Rest assured that "Mirrors" squarely rests within the category of "poorly made crapfest." A woman meets a mirror dwelling ghostie who is accidentally released into the outside world. The problem with this movie is quite simply this: there is too much time wasted on drawn-out, uninteresting, stupid, disgusting events. For example, An ex-cop and his family are the target of an evil force that is using mirrors as a gateway into their home. Are you serious? In yet another scene, he runs through the streets hysterically for an incredibly long period of time.I understand and greatly appreciate the fact that Japanese Remake cyberpunk throws away conventional, formulaic film-making standards and goes for a shot to the heart in terms of a no holds barred visual/audio assault on the senses. That's why I love "Tetsuo: The Iron Man" (directed by Shinya Tsukamoto) and "Rubber's Lover" (directed by Shozin Fukui, the same director of this film) ? they have boatloads of awesome, exceptionally cool visuals for the viewer to chew on. These aren't your typical "crazy" movies with characters acting stupid the entire time. On the contrary, you get wickedly stylish nightmare sequences that blend horrific technology with biological flesh; or Frankenstein-like awakening sequences with psychic antagonists fitted in black rubber suits. Every assault is handled with precision, lasting long enough to leave an impression without provoking irritation. These films are the very essence of successful experimental film-making. Breathtaking stuff! Unfortunately, "Mirrors" reminds us more of Tsai-ming Liang in its habit of shamelessly filming completely pointless, dull-as-dirt moments for incredibly long periods of time. The only difference is that where Tsai chooses to film a person peeing into a plastic bag for 5 minutes, Alexandre Aja chooses to film a woman vomiting profusely for 5 minutes. Both methods will incite record-setting levels of frustration and irritation on the part of the viewer. These types of films are not just poorly made by conventional standards, they're poorly made by ANY standard. Being weird (in and of itself) does not guarantee an entertaining film. "Mirrors" serves to remind us of this.I find it incredibly interesting how so many people fail to apply logical standards to films that fly outside conventional film-making techniques. It would seem that dramas and romances are the only genres of film where viewers go in with a defined set of standards (e.g., acting, story lines, etc.). Trot out a no-brain action movie, and everyone gets confused. Instead of everyone rating those films based on the quality of the action scenes (the only logical way to go), we get snobs trashing every no-brain action film in existence (regardless of how well the action is executed) while fans of the genre rate every movie highly (regardless of how badly the action is executed). In like manner, fans of Wuxia films will never admit that a Chinese swordplay movie made in the 1980s or early 1990s was poorly made, while fans of the Classics seem to think that every film made before 1970 is a "masterpiece." There is something seriously wrong with all of these people.Am I the only living person on the planet who can be a fan of almost every genre of film while maintaining some semblance of quality control? It's really not that difficult to watch and love a Yasujiro Ozu film one night, only to then watch and love a Ron Howard movie the next night. Different standards apply, and while movie-watching is ultimately subjective, viewers would be wise to apply some semblance of standards in every genre. That way, we don't end up mixing garbage like "A Tale of Two Sisters" or "Eraserhead" with truly scintillating fare like "Rubber's Lover" and "Tetsuo: The Iron Man."</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>James Hitchcock</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>Banal, Incoherent and Difficult to Follow</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Hollywood has long regarded the European, especially the British and the French, cinema as a good source on which it can rely whenever its own wells of creative inspiration run dry (as they frequently do). Recently, however, it has widened its geographical reach, at least as far as the horror genre is concerned, and has taken to raiding the Far Eastern cinema in its desperate search for new source material. "Mirrors" is one of a number of Japanese or South Korean horror films which have been remade for American audiences.There has always been something slightly disturbing about mirrors, and the way in which they reflect our world in reverse. Lewis Carroll exploited this disturbing sensation in "Alice through the Looking-Glass", ever since when the expression "looking-glass world" has referred to an inverted or distorted version of reality. "Mirrors" also tries to tap into the sense that a mirror cannot reflect something without distorting it.Ben Carson, a suspended police detective, begins work as a security guard at the Mayflower, a luxury department store which has been closed for several years after being gutted by fire. Ben notices to his surprise that the ruined building still contains numerous mirrors, all apparently undamaged by the fire. Even more strangely, he also begins to notice that these mirrors do not reflect reality exactly; for example, a door which is in reality shut appears open in the mirror. Ben discovers that Gary Lewis, his predecessor as security guard, died in mysterious circumstances. His death was officially regarded as suicide, but Ben begins to suspect that it was connected to the mysterious mirrors.The rest of the plot becomes very complicated and I will not attempt to summarise it in detail. The basic idea is that the department store was built on the site of a former psychiatric hospital and that, as a result of an exorcism carried out on one of the patients, a demon has become trapped in the mirrors. Ben finds out that he and his family are in danger from the demon, which can threaten them not only through the mirrors in the store but through any mirror or surface capable of giving a reflection. To combat the demon, Ben needs to track down the former psychiatric patient, now a nun.The idea of a parallel "mirror-universe" is an intriguing one, and could easily have been adapted for the horror genre. "Mirrors", however, wastes a potentially intriguing opportunity. It hints at the existence of another world which is a distortion of our own, but for the most part the mirror is simply used as a device in a banal, "Exorcist"-type plot about demonic possession of the type that was popular in the seventies but today has been done to death. The film starts off very slowly, with endless shots of Ben walking round the burnt-out building to the accompaniment of spooky music, and when it starts to gather speed the plot becomes incoherent and difficult to follow. About the only distinctive thing about the film is that it features that Hollywood rarity, a racially mixed marriage. (Carson is white and his wife Amy black). Following the success of "24", I would have thought that Kiefer Sutherland could have commanded parts in better films than this one. 3/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Good Until The Last 30 Minutes</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I was really enjoying this movie up until the last half hour or so. It's true that the concept of the world behind the mirror isn't exactly original. I've seen takes on the concept in various places over the years, but there seemed to be a degree of freshness to this. It moved at a pretty good clip, there was a decent enough mystery about this old abandoned department store and it was even frightening at times. I give credit to any movie that manages to give me chills, and this one did from time to time. It opens with a scene that captures your attention right off, with a man cutting his own throat after apparently being chased by something and it moves quickly into the story of Ben Carson (played by Kiefer Sutherland), who takes a job as night security guard in this mysterious building. The movie establishes the right connections - the man who killed himself was Carson's predecessor in the job - and you really are kept watching as it unfolds, with some periodic very graphic and unsettling violence.Sutherland was excellent in the role of Ben, as was Paula Patton as his estranged wife Amy. All was going well - and then came the last half hour, when the mystery of the mirrors was revealed and everything seemed to go downhill pretty quickly, as a pretty good horror movie descended into over the top silliness. Once Anna Esseker was introduced this somehow seemed to lose its edge. As a 6 year old Anna was placed in a psychiatric hospital because of a personality disorder. As part of her treatment, she was put in a mirrored room to force her to confront herself. But it seems that Anna was really possessed by some type of malevolent spirit(s) who somehow moved from her into the mirrors. Here's where it got inexplicably weird. OK. The spirits moved into the mirrors that surrounded Anna in the room in 1952. But we're supposed to believe that they now seem to inhabit every mirror - including those that weren't even made in 1952? And not even just mirrors but that they're also in water or anything else that makes a reflection? I just didn't get that, and the last few minutes of the movie were especially ridiculous, as Anna returns with Ben to the mirrored room (which was still there even after the hospital became a department store, and even after the department store burned down years before) and became re- possessed by the spirits.The last scene of the movie introduces a whole new problem for Ben to try to resolve, although I won't go into any details about that. It could, I suppose, be fodder for a sequel that continues to look at life behind the mirrors. This is really quite good up until the last half hour, which unfortunately left a bad taste in my mouth.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Ever want to see Jack Bauer fight demons? Here ya go...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is one of those movies that has a good preview that catches your attention, but even as you watch the preview you can tell that the movie can't take the idea anywhere interesting. Any elaboration on the intriguing idea will just ruin it. If you thought you saw all the good parts and could skip the movie, you're right. When you watch the preview, and you see the images in the mirror act independently of the people reflected and make threatening faces when the people turn away, and you wonder what is going on and if the explanation will be at all interesting, the answer is no. The more you learn about what's going on, the less interesting it gets.You also see Keifer Sutherland, as a security guard working in a crappy looking darkened building, and you think how it might be nice to see him in a different kind of role, especially after so many seasons of 24.Well, predictably, the film's creators decided not to take too many risks, and Sutherland turns out to be just playing Jack Bauer all over again, like he wandered off the 24 set and crashed a horror film.Yes, here he is, the law enforcement guy with the tormented past and the estranged family. He has knowledge of some threat and no one will believe him. He has to go off the grid, conduct his own personal investigations. and, finally, here he is as a desperate, frantic man, abducting a nun at gunpoint and screaming that he has to save his family. Yes, this is really 24 meets Stigmata.That disappointing direction is the biggest problem, but it is not the only problem. there are too many clichés and tropes, such as the bitchy estranged wife. Sometimes I think every screenwriter in Hollywood is separated and having custody problems and has to work his issues out on film, but I've seen this subplot so many times and I was sick of it from the very beginning. Paula Patton, as Jack Bauer's estranged wife, really lays it on thick. The script calls for her to be intense about everything, all the time whether she is intensely annoyed, concerned, worried, terrified, you name it. She might as well just stand in the middle of the set just screaming whenever she's on screen, because it would have the same effect as her just reading her lines. It's too bad her character didn't actually get killed.The sets are overdone just like Paula Patton's acting. The burned out mall doesn't look anything like a burned out building. It just looks like one big special effect. If they made it look like an actual place, the mirror antics would have been ten times scarier.Sometimes in horror films the protagonists seem selfish and stubborn and responsible for their own problems. If we don't care about them, then they just seem selfish, as in this film. When Kiefer Sutherland is forcing the nun to go with him at gunpoint to save his family, I wasn't convinced that he and his unpleasant wife and snotty kids were worth dragging the nun into their problems. I really wasn't on his side at that poinnt, or for the rest of the movie.</fullReview>

		<username>MovieProductions</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Oh Alex, where did you go wrong?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>** out of (***)Alexandre Aja, probably one of the best modern-day horror directors, has made a masterpiece in horror history ("High Tension") and, potentially, a staple in modern-day remakes. Unfortunately, "Mirrors" is neither a staple nor masterpiece in any genre or sub- genre. While not an abysmal film, it's something Aja could do in his sleep, and it truly shows. Even rating this film a "5" seems a little on the generous side, but I didn't think it was substandard. I appreciate Aja's direction because he wanted to make a truly unique, out of the ordinary story, but alas, he missed the mark. What could have been a simple, yet effective concept resorted to a convoluted, uninteresting mess. Oh Mr. Aja, how hard you have fallen. "Mirrors" revolves around a night watcher of a mirror store, who's also a cop, that hears of several murderous crimes, although the crimes involve themselves. However, is it truly themselves or is it something in the mirrors that make them do this? By first glance, the concept sounds rather intriguing and captivating. You can say that's true about the film after its forty-five minutes.After that, the film descends into convoluted chaos. So, the script, in a nutshell, is very weak. Moreover, the performers are highly unsatisfied. Yes, they aren't stereotypical and unlikable, but they are so irritable in their actions and the decisions they make are very questionable. Furthermore, the direction is very uneven. The first half is very briskly paced, while the second half is drawn-out and slow. Additionally, this film should be excessively gore since Aja is attached to this film right? Wrong. This movie has only two gory scenes, and while those are extremely graphic, it's still not satisfactory. Furthermore, the resolution is seriously one of the worst I've seen. Rather than coming off as cool and intriguing, it comes off as a gimmick and almost like the director thought "how do I end this movie?". While there were flaws in the film, there were some nice aspects of the film. For starters, the cinematography is certainly sharp. Furthermore, this film has an exciting, intense climax which is so epic. Moreover, the two gory scenes in this film are worth a rental. Furthermore, the first half of this film is downright entertaining. Overall, "Mirrors" is a severely disappointing film. What could have been such an intriguing nail-biting concept took a down whirl spiral in a drawn-out, pretentious motion picture. Also, Aja is pretty devoid of plot holes, but this film had "plot holes" written all over it. I don't care how follows this film, you cannot tell me everything, when pieced together, makes total sense. While the gore and cinematography deliver, the performances and script don't. I perhaps it's intriguing enough for a look, but anything more is just a waste of time and money.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Interesting horror movie, with an Asian origin.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Asian cultures have different concepts of spiritual themes, and the stories often make for very interesting movies, like this one.Kiefer Sutherland is Ben Carson, a cop on leave until a death is sorted out. He is married and with two young children, and needs to work, both for the income and the peace of mind. He takes a job as a night watchman for a large, burned-out department store while its disposition awaits the completion of investigations and legal actions. His job is simple, walk through the place every hour with a flashlight to make sure no one is intruding. Otherwise he has a small, comfortable trailer to stay in, adjacent to the building.But not everything is right in there. He hears anguished screams, but no one is actually there. He sees reflections in mirrors that aren't really reflections. He sees hand prints on the mirrors that are "inside" the mirror and can't we wiped off. Soon he finds that his own family members are in danger. So the movie becomes his attempt to use a few clues to track down what is really going on here and especially to save his family from harm.Paula Patton is good as his wife Amy Carson, who also works in the morgue to do autopsies. Amy Smart is good as his sister, Angela Carson.The movie is effectively scary. I started watching it with Natalie, a 21-year-old college student but she had to quit, she was afraid it would give her nightmares. There is quite a bit of explicit blood and gore, but underneath is a pretty good horror story.SPOILERS: Before the big building was a department store it was a hospital. There in 1952 a 12-yr-old girl seemed to be possessed and was treated in a large room of mirrors. Initially thought to have died in a fire in 1952, she actually was transferred 2 days earlier, and she now nearing 70 was a nun in a convent. The spirit that possessed her those years ago had remained, trapped in the mirrors, and Ben had to find her and bring her back to that room to neutralize the evil spirit and save his own family (even though his sister had already died for the evil). In the end, after the building collapsed on him, we see Ben in the NYC streets, apparently OK. But no, he saw everything backwards, he was not a live person anymore, he was now a spirit in the mirrors. No one could see him, but he could make handprints inside mirrors. Creepy!</fullReview>

This is my first exposure to Aja, and it is about what his The Hills Have Eyes appears to be like. For all the potential in the concept, this is fairly straight-forward, and doesn't tread any new ground. This is to working off the inherent creepiness of mirrors, the fact that they "reverse" everything, and that strange sense that there is something more to what they show us than just our reflection. It seems clear that they understood this when making it, but they still did not manage to do anything extraordinary. This is pretty terrifying, if there are a few cheap jump-scares. The atmosphere is built up and maintained nicely, and this can be rather subtle, on occasion. Cinematography and editing are skillful. I gotta say, Sutherland may have been on 24 for too long; he can't entirely shake the Bauer voice. The acting tends to be good, though the kids are utterly unimpressive, which takes away from sequences where their performance is important. This has very well-done effects, and they make an impact, they don't feel as though they're there to show off the technology. It certainly does deliver what one comes to expect from recent entries in the genre; there is plenty of swearing, blood, gore, disgusting sights and eye candy for males. On the plus side, it definitely is also disturbing. The disc includes two informative featurettes, 48 and 18 minutes long, respectively, and 15 minutes of alternate and deleted scenes with an optional, interesting commentary by the director and the writer. I recommend this to big fans of this kind of movie. If you're spellbound by the idea behind this, you're likely to be disappointed by this; it simply does not break the mold. 7/10</fullReview>

		<fullReview>Mirrors, in earlier times polished metal plates, surfaces of stoves, natural stone plates and the like have been suspected to be entrances into a world beyond this our world since the oldest times. So, I cannot agree when a crew member said that the movie's basic purpose was to show how people actually care about their reality. "Our" reality can only be cared for in contrast with another reality. If there is only one reality, there is nothing to compare with.In addition to the well-known geography there is, according to the philosopher Gotthard Günther, something like a "metaphysical geography", which shows that the mirrors and the flat plates are just a subset of a whole world of remote mountain valleys, ravines, hollows, holes, clefts, gaps and other openings which stimulated the fantasy of humankind on the surface of the earth as the ocean did underneath the surface and where ghosts and other supernatural beings were supposed to live and do their pranks. What was obviously not supposed to be populated by humans and animals, had to be populated by fairies, and it was only a question of time until the conquest of this ghosts would start against the humans. Information abolishes the lack of knowledge, but the lack of knowledge creates fear, and hence the main source for the projection mechanisms that populated the world beyond the mirrors.I cannot agree with, as it seems, the majority of people for whom "Mirrors" (2008) is a second-class movie. It has scenes that go even beyond "The Silence of the Lambs". One scene towards the end is a nice reverence to Cronenberg's "Scanners". The slasher-tricks are built in this movie with a consistent sense of the aesthetics of ugliness.</fullReview>

		<date>2009-05-03</date>
		<username>sgtking</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>One of the best horror films of recent years</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I've always felt that one of the scariest devices used in horror films is a mirror. It's been used in many films, sometimes successfully and others not so much. Movies like 'Poltergeist III' and the first two 'Candyman' films are some of the better examples of horror films using mirrors effectively. The idea of seeing something evil in the reflection and then turning and nothing is there is pretty frightening. Remakes have become a big thing in Hollywood lately, especially of horror films including ones from Asia. In this case, it's a remake of the 2003 Korean film 'Into the Mirror,' which I have yet to see, but would very much like to. This is the second remake done by 'High Tension' creator Alexandre Aja and though I liked his version of 'The Hills Have Eyes' I must admit that his latest is truly one of the best and most entertaining horror films of the decade.Pros: Excellent work done by the cast. A really neat opening titles sequence. Beautifully shot and some really nice shots of New York City. Great score that's good and chilling in all the right places. Gruesome deaths. A fair amount of blood and some gore. Plenty creepy moments, images, and atmosphere. Well-paced. Excellent make-up effects and good CGI. Director/co-writer Aja does a great job of creating suspense and maintaining it most of the way through. An ending you'll either love or hate, but definitely won't forget.Cons: The explanation for where the evil came from is a bit underwhelming. Does feel a tad overlong.Final thoughts: Maybe not one of the scariest horror films of all times, but after so many mediocre remakes and PG-13 horror films this is a real breath of fresh air. Aja did fine with his first two films and with each film he's gotten even better and this one is no exception. This is one of the reasons why I eagerly await his next film, 'Piranha 3-D.' My rating: 4.5/5</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>Remakes, per se, I can live without. Sure, I understand the desire to cash in on a tried and true formula or a current trend- but, as I've pointed out several times, now, elsewhere in these comments, I think that there are more than enough untapped sources out there, from short stories to novels, that there need never be another remake. And, if one were morally compelled to remake a movie, remake something that wasn't done quite well enough the first time around, a real stinker. (Of which there have been more than a few...) But second hand is still second hand. (Which is why I refuse to see the remake of (REC) until I've had the chance to see the original.) Despite my reservations, I gave MIRRORS a look (yuck, yuck). 		<username>Jonny_Numb</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>The Reflecting Dog</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Someday, I want Alexandre Aja to be the type of esteemed director who leads a new vanguard in the craftsmanship of cinematic fear. Films like "High Tension" and the high-voltage remake of "The Hills Have Eyes" certainly affirmed his knack for blending character, suspense, and sanguine splatter with dexterous ease, and seemed to be sounding the alarm for a new, exciting talent. And it's not like "Mirrors" is a terrible film per se: the production design is brilliant, atmospheric, and brooding; the acting is capable; and, for a while, the story?despite its inherent ridiculousness?manages to captivate. But the problem with making a movie about possessed, killer mirrors comes in the telling: if you're too serious or light in tone about it, it will elicit laughs where there should be none. And "Mirrors"?much like "Tension" and "Hills"?is a deathly brooding, humorless affair that tumbles into silliness time and again. While I gave Aja the benefit of the doubt for about 3/4 of the running time (even overlooking some of the more laughable elements?including a "painting over" montage), once the source of the murderous mayhem is uncovered, the film dives into hoary cliché, replete with CG demon-faces, half-baked religious imagery, collapsing buildings, and explosions. With the backing of a major studio, Aja's tendencies shift more toward bombastic spectacle than a fitting conclusion. And the lead performance by Kiefer Sutherland, while serviceable, lacks the depth of character necessary to fully invest us in his plight. It's a shame, really?"Mirrors" had the potential for greatness, which makes its resulting mediocrity all the more disappointing.5.5 out of 10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Spoiler</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This comment contains a spoiler. I didn't catch the goofs in continuity, etc., listed here already, but I did catch one I didn't see mentioned here. In the final scene, as Ben discovers he is trapped inside a mirror's reflection of the town (signs read in reverse,etc.) he crosses the street to get to the store window. Unless this is supposed to be occurring in England, the traffic is on the right side of the street not the left, as it should be in a reflection. How could this have gone unnoticed? In America the lane of oncoming traffic is to the driver's left. In a mirror's reflection, it would appear to be on the right side. Drivers sit on the left side. In a mirror they would appear to be on the right side.</fullReview>

		<date>2009-01-18</date>
		<username>M. T.</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>A Story With A Lot of Potential (but still feels like a rough draft)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Mirrors, like so many horror movies today, has a great premise: the world beyond reflections is, in fact, another world (seemingly inhabited by the spirits of the dead). Cool. Throw in a possessed child (who ends up being a possessed nun)scampering around a dark and dingy department store basement, and you would think that all the ingredients for a great horror tale are there - and they are! However, the story seems like a rehearsal, or a rough draft story that needs polishing. It was enjoyable and I recommend it to horror fans, but what is most frustrating is thinking that it could have been TRULY great. Three suggestions: Keep the story in the dark of the abandoned department store and its underworld. Focus on the tale of the possessed child/nun and let her inhabit the building in a Changeling/Exorcist manner. Lose the "my family is in danger" aspect and keep it simple: lone security guard in peril in the building (perhaps even keep the tale to one night). Okay who cares what I think, I know, but I just had to rant a bit. Now for the faults: why are they painting out the mirrors with green paint as if afterward they can clean them off? Anyone in real life would have taken them down and put them in the basement or something. Obviously a necessary gimmick to allow the entity to return later, only further revealing the rough draft quality of story-telling here. Further, we are never fully aware of why the "entity" in the mirrors so desperately wanted the little girl (nun) back. Also, the "kills" seem a bit gratuitous and simple - as if to keep the teens watching. Skip them - or give them more basis than "I am an angry demon". And FINALLY, I felt there was a lot of potential for atmospheric CREEP factor that was lost: skip the bloody introduction which reveals all; unveil the tale with patience...nothing is more frightening than the unseen or only alluded-to. I sat wishing that the beginning of the movie had been Sutherland's initial entrance in the creepy store...then spent the rest of the movie thinking how much better it could have been in general. Loved the final scene, however! It was a shocker.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Starts out interesting, but logic takes a leap to the mirror world in the second half.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Reader be warned: when a movie's trailers shows its only two good scenes, you're in for trouble.Mirrors follows the adventures of Ben Carson (Kiefer Sutherland, who unfortunately has forgotten how to act like anybody other than Jack Bauer), a down on his luck police officer with an estranged wife, an attractive younger sister, and the world's most dangerous job: guarding mirrors in a burnt-out department store from hooligans. All nice and normal...until he sees images in the mirrors that should not be there, and that follow him home. Trying to find out what is going on, Ben digs into the past of the department store, and what he finds turns his world upside down (or should that be in reverse? Mirror joke, there). He must then stop the horror before it consumes him and his family.Okay, SPOILERS AHEAD. Fairly warned be thee.The movie starts out creepily enough, and the two horrible deaths are very creative and cool looking, but without those scenes what we have left is the same old 'Ghost Story' in the lines of 'The Grudge,' 'The Ring,' and so on (that is, a PG-13 story with an R rating to lure in the unsuspecting). We have characters being menaced by a demonic force that originally possessed a little girl in the 1940s, that now apparently kills just because it feels like it, with no rhyme or reason. Which leads to the logic issues. Why would it possess the little girl it chooses in the first place? Why did it leap into the mirrors when it got the chance? Why does it kill very visibly and violently, when it could give the characters heart attacks and hide for years without detection? Why does it need the little girl, grown up after 60 years, to possess again, when it seems like half the other people in the movie would work just as well? Having this remain 'mysterious' is foolish: they are reasonable questions, and given the film has nearly two hours to answer them and doesn't isn't a good sign.And don't get me started about the ending scenes. I think this very well may be the first movie I've ever seen where a nun, who acts very sensibly and reasonably, gets coerced at gunpoint into fighting a demon, does so without complaint, and then when she is possessed, gets yelled at and beat up by Kiefer Sutherland.Yes, that's right: See Kiefer Sutherland set a nun on fire! If there weren't spoiler issues, I would have made that my summary right there. If that line made you shake your head, don't see the movie. If it made it you laugh, you might give the movie a glance. It's certainly not the worst movie of this genre, as long as you don't think too much about it. Just be assured it does NOT do for mirrors what Psycho did for showers.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Mirror, mirror on the wall....</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Dr. Morris: "When one starts to perceive one's own reflection as a completely separate being, one is suddenly confronted with two entirely separate egos, two entirely separate worlds that can strip us at any given moment. Feeling of self-hatred, usually triggered by a psychological shock, can split the personality in two, hence creating two or more personalities with distinct memories with distinct behavior patterns within the same individual. The patient then has the false perception of the existence of two distinct worlds. The real world, and the world inside a mirror."Kiefer Sutherland stars as an NYPD detective-turned-security guard who discovers something sinister lurking in the mirrors of a fire-damaged department store in Haute Tension writer/director Alexandre Aja's menacing study in the origins of evil. It's been just about a year since mercurial police detective Ben Carson (Sutherland) was suspended from the NYPD for the fatal shooting of an undercover officer, and ever since that fateful day he's been locked in a self-destructive spiral of anger and alcoholism. Increasingly isolated from his wife and kids, Ben spends most nights crashed-out at his sister Angela's (Amy Smart) apartment in Queens. But Ben hasn't given up hope just yet, and in order to get his life back together and prove that he's still capable of supporting his family he takes a job as the night watchman at the Mayflower department store. The Mayflower used to be a lavish symbol of inner-city prosperity, that is, until a raging inferno gutted the building while claiming numerous lives in the process. These days, the Mayflower is a scorched reminder of human misery, the ornate mirrors therein reflecting a suffering so profound that it begins to wear on Ben's already-fragile psyche. Not only that, but whatever force dwells behind the shimmering glass seems to have gained the power to alter reality as well.After Ben gazes into the mirrors and sees a vision of himself being relentlessly tortured, he is horrified to experience violent convulsions, spontaneous bleeding, and frightening asphyxiation. And while his sister is always willing to lend a sympathetic ear, she chalks the anomalies up to an unusually potent mix of stress and anxiety. Unfortunately for Ben, his estranged wife, Amy (Paula Patton), isn't nearly as forgiving. A prosaic NYPD medical examiner who has seen her fair share of tragedy, Amy fears that Ben's erratic behavior could be placing their children in danger. Later, as Ben begins to draw connections between his increasingly gruesome visions and a former Mayflower security guard who vanished without a trace, he begins to suspect that an unimaginable evil is using the mirrors as a gateway into the real world, and that his family is in mortal danger from forces beyond their realm of understanding. Perhaps if he Ben can manage to convince Amy that their children's lives are at risk, he can summon the courage to face the greatest evil he has ever known.I'm in a pickle in this one. A part of me says that I really liked it and I was scared enough to like it but a part of me also says it sucked, the second act dragged the movie down and the demon battle scene in the end was totally unnecessary and hilarious at the same time. To be honest, that bit was a turn-off, it was going so well then the old lady with flying ninja skills had to pop up and battle Jack Bauer. But at least, the surprising (for lack of a better word) ending was a shocker and a pretty decent conclusion to end it all.Alexandre Aja, the director of The Hills Have Eyes remake and High Tension, delivers all the goods in the first act, the second act kind of slows down losing the spooky vibe and becoming more of a monster movie but tops it off with a pretty decent ending.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>STAR RATING: ***** Saturday Night **** Friday Night *** Friday Morning ** Sunday Night * Monday Morning New York cop Ben Carson (Kiefer Sutherland) quit the force after an undercover assignment went tragically wrong. He takes a job as a night watch man on the reconstruction site of a burned down department store to get by. However, one thing he notes is the really large mirrors there- and from then on, whenever he looks in the mirror, it's as if some sinister force has taken hold of his life and he's driven to desperate lengths to find out why and to stop it.Kiefer Sutherland spent years in the acting wilderness, but spent the best part of the noughties developing an iconic figure of his own in the shape of Agent Jack Bauer with the hit TV show 24. Still a hit series today, the man has enough clout to get a lead role in a big studio production like Mirrors. But by choosing projects as piecemeal as this, his star may continue to shine brightest on TV.The DVD of the film offers the theatrical version or the far gorier 18 rated uncut version, which I, always preferring the fuller picture, chose to watch. Outrageous and gratuitous gore, though, will only perk things up for the most shallow viewers. The clunky script and hollow dialogue are like sketching paper for the formulaic, by the numbers film Mirrors is. It's not predictable and there is some strong mystery behind the curse of the mirrors, but the revelation at the end is just ridiculous and baffling and there is no genuine feeling of tension or suspense through any of it. Even more unintentionally funny are Sutherland's sister's death and the 'spooky' bit where he redundantly fires bullets at the mirrors which show no damage. The film pretentiously and presumptuously tries to round itself off with a Sixth Sense style, profound ending which turns the story on it's head but the effect is too transparent to make any impact.Not an awful little horror film at all, just an unremarkable and preposterous one, best served for an in flight movie on a red eye flight home. **</fullReview>

		<username>Paul Andrews (poolandrews@hotmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>A film of two halves.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Mirrors is set in New York where ex cop Ben Carson (executive producer Kiefer Sutherland) has to take a job as a nighttime security guard at the old burned down department store ruins called the Mayflower to support himself &amp; his estranged wife &amp; kids. While patrolling the Mayflower ruins Carson starts seeing things in the mirrors which aren't really there, he sees himself on fire &amp; other people badly burned screaming for help. Carson comes to believe that the mirrors inside the Mayflower store are possessed by evil spirits that want him to find someone called Esseker, Carson calls a few favors in on the force &amp; learns Esseker was a young patient at the psychiatric hospital where the Mayflower store now stands, a hospital with a dark past &amp; an equally dark future unless Carson can find a way to break the curse &amp; free the spirits...Worked on under the title Into the Mirror this American Romanian co-production was co-written &amp; directed by Axelandre Aja for all intents &amp; purposes Mirros is a remake of the South Korean supernatural horror film Into the Mirror &amp; is one in a long line of American remakes of Asian horror films along with the likes of The Ring (2002), The Grudge (2004), Dark Water (2005), Pulse (2006), The Eye (2008) &amp; One Missed Call (2008) all of which have been of variable quality. Mirrors as it stands falls somewhere in the middle, while not a total waste of time it really isn't that great or satisfying a film. Mirrors does start off quite nicely with a cool kill scene &amp; the introduction of Sutherland's character, this opening half is when the films at it's best with a nice mood &amp; atmosphere while the story is also still intriguing at this point too. Unfortunately once all the nonsense about the psychiatric hospital kicks in director Aja forgets all about the horror aspects &amp; Mirrors becomes some sort of silly supernatural thriller with Carson kidnapping a nun in Pennsylvania at gunpoint to try &amp; save his family. The story is never really explained, there's not enough meat on the bones as it's something to do with schizophrenia &amp; these demons trapped in the mirrors want to get out &amp; once they do we are treated to the extremely silly sight of some old zombie witch thing &amp; Sutherland fighting it out in some tunnels, there's no rhyme or reason to it &amp; the ending is just a bit embarrassing although I will the final few minutes do offer up a pretty cool twist ending that almost saves it. Too many things are left unanswered, like how did that earlier security know to send those newspaper clippings to Carson? Why did he send them? The rules are never really defined, it's stated that the images in the mirror can make you do things but then that annoying kid Michael is sucked into the floor &amp; it just feels like the rules of the film change at a whim. Also at over 100 minutes long it does drag especially when all the scares &amp; tension is abandoned for silly explanations &amp; exposition.I watched this on Blu-ray last night &amp; selected the 'Extended Cut' which apparently contains a bit more gore, the real gory scenes are the initial throat slashing, someones pulls their own jaw off &amp; there's a couple of scenes set inside a morgue where some bodies can be seen. When Sutherland is being spooked by what he sees in the mirrors early in the film Mirrors is quite creepy &amp; rather effective &amp; it's a shame what the film turns into later on. French director Aja likes his remakes as his first US film was The Hills Have Eyes (2006) remake, his second was this remake &amp; his third is set to be Piranha 3-D (2010) which is a remake of Joe Dante's classic killer fish flick Piranha (1978).With a supposed budget of about $35,000,000 this had a lukewarm reception at the box-office both commercially &amp; critically which is understandable,although set in New York this was partly filmed in Romania. The film looks good with nice production values &amp; decent enough effects work. The acting is alright, Sutherland is always watchable although his two kids &amp; ex wife are awful &amp; irritated me.Mirrors isn't a total waste but doesn't build on it's creepy first half as it should &amp; that ending fight between Sutherland &amp; that witch is just ridiculous. Could have been better.</fullReview>

		<username>areitman07</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>My Favorite Nightmare</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Mirrors are standard fare for horror junkies, such as myself. I think that most little kids have some sort of 'thing' with them. After all, who hasn't stood in front of a mirror, with the lights out, and whispered "bloody Mary, bloody Mary...""? And who hasn't scampered away before the completion? I know for sure that I have, and so have most of my friends. And I can't count the number of truly creepy horror movies that utilize mirrors to one extent or another.The reason is apparent. To a creative mind, mirrors are more than just glass. They are where you see the one person you cannot see: Yourself. They are purveyors of perception. They are doorways to alternate realities, as far back as Lewis Carroll, or further."Mirrors" contains many of the creepy elements that make mirrors scary. My favorite nightmare (and a recurring one from age 6 to age 12) was when I would look in a mirror and I would move and my reflection would not. I told this to the barrista at Arclight, before I went into the theatre and he smiled knowingly. OK. So, I guess I'll be having nightmares tonight! But there is enough gore and ick-factor, to keep the film from being so menacing that I slept with the lights on. (Paradoxically, bloody junk usually lightens this type of film.)And, on top of it, Keifer Sutherland brings in enough Jack Bauer to give me the 24 fix I've been craving! How can I have terrible nightmares when Jack's around?? The film most certainly does have a lot of nightmarish elements, though, and the back story is well-played, if a bit cliché. Additionally, much of the film work is lovely, drawing the viewer's attention where it needs to be, without using a cattle-prod.All said, I liked the film and did not have nightmares... but I did wake up in the middle of the night and refuse to leave my husband's side, just in case the mirror in the bathroom was holding a grudge.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2008-08-29</date>
		<username>Cruiz Dwyer (sinncross@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>A little old school, a little modern, though not entirely complete...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Ben Carson (Keifer Sutherland) is currently undergoing marital problems with his wife, Amy (Paula Patton), who is not showing much interest in his wellbeing. As an attempt to stabilize his life, Ben lands a job as a nighttime security guard for an abandoned, yet important, department store. However Ben comes across some mysterious circumstances when it appears that the mirrors in the building are reflecting an alternative reality to his own.The story seems like a simple affair at first: man goes around some dark and mysterious place, discovers something he shouldn't, and all hell breaks loose. For the sakes of everyone, the film does attempt to be more then that. Director Alexandre Aja does a good job at meshing both the supernatural elements of the mirrors with the reality of Carson's married life. The former is tense with some some great moments marred by some which are clichéd. The overall result is hardly mixed however, and proves to serve horror in a good dose, and as well has a nice hint of Asian horror. Like the horror, the social elements either work or do not. The conflict between both Ben and Amy seems rooted in reality and can be related to. But the film does little in the beginning arc to help attach the viewer to Ben's social life circumstances and so there are a few scenes which don't quite gel well enough to be entirely believable. The overall story moves at a decent enough pace, slowly bringing to light new point for the viewer. But after the two-thirds mark, the story not only picks up speed but content as well to the point where the last third of the film has enough story to be told in the first two-thirds. The problem is that too much happens towards the end that its difficult to justify cohesion in the films plot. It really needed to be a few minutes longer so that some of the plot could get more fleshing out.But what is presented, Aja does a great job directing. The cinematography is not exceptional by any means, but is still a wonder to watch. The best of the lot easily goes to the death sequences, with the first being an excellent prologue into the story, and another which is truly numbingly evil: you'll want to keep watching due to its uniqueness but at the same time turn your eyes away. Sadly not all scenes are well thought through, mainly due to the story. The ending, for instance, was rather surprising but apt. However there is no underlying logic to link the events to any tangible truth expressed through Aja's direction. And this is easily the films greatest fault: Aja appears to hope the audience will not question how the film progresses. Even the explanation for the mirrors is still left shrouded, and not because its all expected to be a big mystery, but because Aja does little to use his directional cue to help give more explanation when its needed.Acting is commendable with a few solid performances but none of them are truly able to be anything but good. Sutherland pulls the film along and is fairly believable throughout as a confused security guard going through marital problems, while having to cope with the mystery that surrounds the mirrors. The only downside is that his performance does, at times, bear a slight resemblance to his role in 24. It's nothing major, and will perhaps only be available to ardent fans of the series. Other characters are minor, though Patton does get a nice little chunk and is adequate enough to support Sutherland, but when the script seems to falter with her character, so does Patton. Thankfully her acting is not unbearable.No sex, and some slight nudity, the film is loaded with scattered with swearing, though not always hard. The violence does get gory with two particular scenes, shown in the red-band trailer, being excessively so. And the camera lingers on the bodily wounds and accompanied blood. Beyond this there is nothing exceedingly graphics about the scenes, with the worst being the images of people on fire but this is achieved with poor CG and so lessens its effect. In fact, fans of Aja's work will possibly be a tad disappointed that the gore level never reaches his previous efforts, notably The Hills Have Eyes, but that doesn't stop it from being powerful images nonetheless.Mirrors is very much like a mirror in all honesty. There is the real, tangible film, but then also the diluted image which is created from the reflection: as much as the film seems to promote substance, it inadvertently at times does not. The story is interesting, though inconsistent. The acting is good, though not entirely exclusive. The horror has some excellent moments, though there probably is not enough. Regardless, Mirrors is not a poor excuse for a film. On the contrary, its a good attempt and quite enjoyable. If anything, Mirrors proves an excellent introduction for those who have not accustomed themselves, or are not willing to do so, to the excessive gore of modern horror.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Great Movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Great MovieAfter reading some of the other less-than-enthusiastic user reviews I have to say that I only slightly agree with some of their conclusions. Sure, there were some "so-so" moments, and holes in the storyline, but on a whole I thought this was an A-Class horror/thriller ? one of the best I've ever seen. After all, it's just a movie. Thus, I tend to base my judgments on how well it keeps my attention, how they effect me, and not by what I would have done differently. And, by how many times it makes my wife jump. She reports having been startled at least 10 times and it never lost my attention. That's pretty darn good. The look of this movie is creepy and often disturbing ? sort of Silent Hillish ? which, along with The Ring, seems to have become the norm for horror movies. I loved the look, feel, and creepiness of the massive burned-out department store and thought it was very well done. Overall, the movie seemed like a combination of The Ring, Silent Hill, The Frighteners, and The Boogyman. In fact, the film's opening sequence in the subway could have come right out of the video game Silent Hill 3. The main character played by Kiefer Sutherland, is an ex-cop named Ben who is waiting to be reinstated after killing someone in the line of duty, and is just all screwed up emotionally and every-other way. Sutherland does an excellent job ? as always. A good supporting cast made this film even better. I would swear even Jack from the series 24 made an appearance or two. But while the first half or ? of the movie is genuinely terrifying, some of the rest becomes somewhat standard stuff. This is a small criticism since I enjoyed the entire film; but once Sutherland hooks up with the old nun the movie seems to take on a, "well, let's get this over with" feel. But all is forgiven with what can only be described as a great ending which left it wide open for a sequel ? or not. There are fantastically creepy atmospherically theatrical elements throughout that were definitely intended for the big screen, especially inside the massive burned out store. But the main strength of the movie is when Ben explores, and eventually witnesses, insanely disturbing and creepy events inside of the store. There are plenty of charred mannequins which seemed to follow Ben's every move, but the creepiest events are mainly associated with large mirrors. Reflections of seemingly innocent victims, which I figured represented supernatural replays of customers caught in the fire, are seen burning alive; grotesque people lying on the floors covered in burns and bloody wounds were crying for help, and distant disembodied screams, sent genuine chills of pending dread up my spine. Of course, all of this happens while Ben's working the night shift in the dark with only the aid of a flashlight. The large movie theater screen effect makes these scenes unbelievably striking. If it's not seen in the theater, I'd definitely urge it be viewed on as large a big screen as possible. My 65-inch screen might be the smallest able to adequately reflect the massive creepy atmosphere of the department store effectively ? that, of course, is just my opinion. But, there is so much detail to be missed on smaller screens. I'd also recommend at the least a home theater 5.1 surround system ? but again, that's just me. In all, I give this film a 9 out of 10.</fullReview>

The ending twist will not blow your mind, but it is portrayed well, linking together earlier imagery. Despite being another remake, the actors and director help set the film slightly above the avalanche of cloned films out there right now.</fullReview>

		<username>gavin6942</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Good Summer 2008 Movie Considering the Options</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Kiefer Sutherland plays Ben Carson, a police detective who is kicked off the force for killing another officer. Now he is forced to live with his little sister Angela (Amy Smart) and work as a night watchman for a burned down department store, the Mayflower. While there, he stumbles upon an evil force that resides in the mirrors and will stop at nothing to hurt him and his family.I would love to compare this to the original Korean version, but I can't as I haven't seen it. Thus, I must enter into this assuming the story is for the most part original, even though I suspect it's not. It was an awkward film for Alexandre Aja, who thrilled us with "High Tension" and brought new life to "Hills Have Eyes". Aja works well with blood and guts and raw intensity, but it seems that trying to work with dialog and family situations is his weakness: line after line in this movie seemed cheesy or forced. The son, in particular, was a very poor actor.Luckily, Aja has some gore moments. We start with a throat-slitting scene. There's a very bloody scene with Amy Smart, a scene that probably is the highlight of the film. There's a few other jumps and scares... with some CG fire thrown in. Not necessary to be CG, but not as annoying as it could have been. More gore and action would have helped this picture, which drags a bit and plays to Aja's weakness. And the plot gets a bit weaker as it unravels, with the "secrets" of the mirrors not being all that interesting for me.I'm always happy to see Amy Smart, and for those who like skin you'll get some here. (Personally I think she looks better with clothes on, but I'm weird.) And Kiefer Sutherland, who probably hasn't done horror since "The Lost Boys" (unless you count "Dark City"), was as likable as ever. Playing an ex-cop might have been a bit close to his role as Jack Bauer on "24", but I think that's what many people want. (I have never seen "24".) If you see only one horror movie in theaters this summer, I guess "Mirrors" is a worthy choice. It's pretty much this one or "Midnight Meat Train", which also had its flaws. Maybe see both and together they'd be equal to one good film. In short, "Mirrors" has a lot of potential and some good scenes, but is largely easy to pass up and the weakest spot on Aja's (short) filmography.</fullReview>

		<date>2008-08-19</date>
		<username>Craig McPherson</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>Mirror mirror on the wall, is this the most boring movie of them all?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Consistency, it would seem, isn't one of Alexandre Aja's fortes. Between 2003 and 2006, on the strength of his breakthrough Haute Tension (High Tension/Switchblade Romance) and remake of The Hills Have Eyes, the Parisian director found himself ensconced as a charter member of the "splat pack" alongside such horror notables as Darren Lynn Bousman, Neil Marshall, Greg Mclean, Eli Roth, James Wan, Leigh Whannell, and Rob Zombie. However whereas many of his contemporaries have continued to mature and refine their talent, Aja has found himself stuck in neutral of late, serving as screenwriter for the merely passable P2, and now missing the mark completely with Mirrors, a film on which he doubled up as writer and director.Based on the 2003 Korean film "Into the Mirror", Kiefer Sutherland stars as Ben Carson, a former New York City police detective whose history of alcoholism and wrongful shooting of an innocent during a previous investigation has him struggling to put his life back together. Carson's inner demons, coupled with his landing a job as a night watchman in a burned out department store whose mirrors appear to play tricks on the mind, hint at a promising tale designed to blur the line between reality and psychosis. Instead, Aja and screen writing partner Grégory Levasseur completely drop the ball. Early on they establish Carson's dependence on powerful prescription medication to curb his desire to drink. They also show him stumbling upon a bottle of whiskey during his first night in his watchman's cabin, yet they fail to use these plot points as anything more than red herrings, instead opting to spin a story about a malevolent supernatural force trapped in the store's mirrors and bent on escape. Sure it might be faithful to the Korean original, but clearly there was room for improvement that was passed over.I'm starting to wonder if the lure of large Hollywood budgets and marquee value stars has conspired to make this talented director grow lazy. Even his penchant for eye catching visuals is spoiled here as the interior sets appear to be a blatant re-hash of those used in 2006's See No Evil. And don't get me started about his reliance on pigeons fluttering for cheap scares - a tactic resorted to so many times I lost count.The end result is a tepid tale that's largely devoid of chills as it aspires to be yet another shoddy Exorcist variant, all of which is too bad given the possibilities of what could have been.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Predictably charmed</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Preface: I'm in the middle of a project where I take a look at formations of awareness, ontologies of the conscious space. A sidetrip, not fully fleshed in writing at this point, involves looking at the manifestation of horror, the rising impulse in mind.Okay, many will attest that Aja is a profoundly sloppy thinker, but now and then he tries to formulate cinematic expression from a conceptual basis. Haute Tension was bungled in the way described by most viewers, his produced Maniac is much more interesting; note there the killing camera. And this. Here, the idea is that a man has to cope with madness.Not vaguely so, but specifically the rising manifestation: the horror comes because he is not able to place horrific images, he mistakenly thinks the mirrored apparitions (the images) do not just reflect mind but are ontologically real 'in' the mirrors, reflecting real unseen fabric. So they become real, acquiring the believed story. In the end, he is trapped in the other side of the looking glass. Okay so the setup is too simple for my taste, a nightwatchman patrols an old shopping mall full of mirrors. The demonic angle is a wholesale loan. And halfway through, a psychiatrist shows up to explain in clinical terms, which further confirms Aja's problem: he is not working from a deep intuition, but merely repeats found ideas. And psychologically the idea is outmoded, positing 'inner' and 'outer'. Most viewers who center on the predictability of the horror and ideas are right to take a punishing look at the film. But I am ever so slightly charmed by the possibilities herein.</fullReview>

When he hears creepy noises or screams instead of running he decides to go investigate it. And thus digs himself into a deeper hole. This might have worked better as a video game cause at least you would be able to control the character walking around with a flash light and a gun. But that isn't the case and it's boring. This is a remake of a Korean horror movie "Into The Mirror". Which I haven't checked out yet, but pretty sure it's better this this crappy flick. Near the end it was alright but it really isn't worth sitting through the whole time to get to it. Even the dialogue and scenarios unintentionally comes off a bit humorous than serious. Everything is just predictable and not really scary or has any tension. It's just not a recommendable horror flick.3.5/10</fullReview>

		<username>Coventry</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Warning: Objects in the Mirror are more EVIL than they appear</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Barely five years ago and especially in horror circles, the name Alexadre Aja pretty much was synonym with "prodigy", "breath of fresh horror air" and even "saviour of a dying genre". Of course, this was right after he came from nowhere and suddenly delivered the downright brilliant and genuinely shocking "High Tension". What a terrific film that was, and finally a true horror movie again that held the power to upset and petrify. Obviously, Aja's one-way ticket to Hollywood was a fact, but even there his first accomplishment ? a wildly outrageous remake of "The Hills Have Eyes" ? was a big success. One movie later, however, and Aja already lost most of his sharp movie-making capacities. "Mirrors" is yet another remake, but of an Asian ghost thriller instead of a 70's grindhouse classic this time. Basically this means the film isn't even half as exciting or gory and the screenplay risks to become tedious, confusing and ? worst of all ? infantile and politically correct. The film still nevertheless contains a handful of grim and beautifully suspenseful moments (especially during the first half) and unquestionably any other director would have messed it up even worse, but overall "Mirrors" is the umpteenth mediocre and unmemorable remake of an Asian ghost flick that wasn't even too good to begin with. Some of the positive elements of "Mirrors" include the casting and several of the set pieces. Like few other actors, Kiefer Sutherland can depict a mentally tormented protagonist and here he receives good support from the two beautiful ladies Amy Smart and Paula Patton. Sutherland plays a former cop who, due to alcohol abuse and personal issues, is forced to accept a new job as the night guard in an eerie warehouse that tragically burned out completely five years ago. Strangely enough, all the mirrors in the building are still intact and they appear to conceal a dark and macabre secret. Ben becomes obsessed with the mirrors and starts digging into the sinister past of the building. His family thinks Ben's going completely crazy, but soon enough the evil force breaks through the mirrors of the parental house as well. The first half hour of "Mirrors" is intriguing and extremely promising. Sutherland's character is complex (battling his own demons of the mind and all?) and the ruined warehouse building is a perplexing horror location. There are even some grueling bits of nasty gore in the first half, like a slit throat and a really disgusting sequence in the bathroom. But then the story becomes just too absurd, with the pursuit of 1950's mental hospital patients and nuns living in solitude. The climax inside the destroyed Mayflower warehouse is spectacular and flashy, but substantially void and quite dumb. The conclusion of the film raises more questions than it answers, but simultaneously it really isn't your standard type of Hollywood ending. Perhaps Aja still had some of his original French blood rushing through his veins at the moment of shooting. I'm really curious of what he will make of his next project; a reworking of Dante's creature-feature classic "Piranha".</fullReview>
	</review>
