		<shortDescription>Bauer through the Looking Glass...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>*** This review is courtesy of Fundulgence.com - the One to watch. It also featured on BBC Radio Sheffield, Leeds and York. *** Kiefer Sutherland takes time out of his busy 24 filming/Parole schedule to star in this US unofficial adaptation of Japanese Horror movie 'Into the Mirror'. He plays a shamed LA cop and recovering alcoholic who takes a job working security for a burnt out department store, only to experience sinister visions in the surviving mirrors. He soon finds that the mysterious goings-on aren't limited to his work place and though greeted with scepticism, he fights to protect his family."Some of the inherent scariness is lost in the transition?" Mirrors bears all of the hallmarks of J-horror remakes, like 'the Ring' and 'the Grudge' before it, with the film tapping into universal fears and twisting them into an inescapable, relentless evil, whilst throwing in snapshots of distressing imagery along the way. Like its contemporaries though, some of the inherent scariness is lost in the transition, presumably in trying to make it more accessible to western audiences. Also, whilst there are some effectively eerie scenes, the frights that follow don't come nearly as frequently as they should, the plot devotes more time to the shallow detective work as the history of the building and its sinister events are unearthed."?events are freaky enough to catch Bauer on the back foot" French director Alexandre Aja - who also directed 'The Hills have Eyes' remake - has made a shrewd move casting Sutherland as the focus of the film. His character here is indistinguishable from 24's Jack Bauer (bar a few scars) -even down to the clothes he wears, so character building is largely discarded as unnecessary and if events are freaky enough to catch Bauer on the back foot, then it can only boost the fear factor can't it? It doesn't do Sutherland any favours though; he's even more likely to be typecast after 6 seasons of 24 and this on his CV over the past half decade."It's not for the squeamish" There are some twists and turns along the way, and it's not for the squeamish, but Mirrors is formulaic horror fluff. All the usual ingredients are present, the haunted house, small child possessed, and the disbelief in the film's protagonist (is it all in his head?) and you can't help but feel as though you've seen it all before, and done better.On reflection, this is may be an alternative to the next re-tread of the Saw franchise this Halloween, but it's standard stuff and Kiefer Sutherland starred in much better horror films 20 years ago ? why not rent out the Lost Boys or a 24 box set instead?</fullReview>

		<date>2008-10-15</date>
		<username>Jamie Ward</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>Mirrors can be genuine skin-crawl material, yet these moments are too few and far between.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This year has been an odd one for the horror genre; so far this year alone we have been attacked by numerous unsuspecting serial killers ranging from angry vegetation and vengeful cell phones to heartbroken cameras out to prove a point and dodgy eyeball transplants. And so it only feels natural that we should continue this assembly line of ridiculous plots with another repackage of most of the movies mentioned above, which were also repackages; Mirrors fails to differentiate itself for the most part from its gimmicky companions, throwing together another extremely loose, overwritten bag of bones story that is far more effective at drawing laughs than scares. Yet the movie doesn't quite succumb to the low levels that some have reached this year, mainly through a decent middle act that showcases the lead man's talent at portraying a man slowly slipping into insanity. However, lumbered with a ridiculous opening act and an ending that mauls whatever is left of this corpse of a movie into something even dental records wouldn't help, the effort, although a little way away from being complete nonsense, still never makes it out of the ditch it digs itself merely from trying to convince us that this is even possible, scary or interesting.I've always believed that the greatest horror movies do one thing and one thing only; place an interesting character or set of characters into a situation in which they have no real control. Unfortunately however, as has been the case with the majority of the genre's output from the past decade, usually we are given a mystery-driven plot fuelled by clues that in the end help to solve the riddle which is behind all the paranormal murders. Through this, our protagonists find themselves in a sticky situation and from there go out to the supermarket, buy a crate of jam and begin piling more onto what they began with. One thing that Mirrors does do that is out of the ordinary however is that it drags TV star Kiefer Sutherland in to play lead. Yes, that's right, a man. Sure, it's superficial on paper; it shouldn't really matter whether the lead character is male or female, and yet it sure does feel refreshing to have someone who isn't in their twenties, overly vulnerable and always clutching to a grey cardigan who is out solving our newest paranormal game of Cluedo.Of course, much of his character and Sutherland too, is wasted on the trite that passes for a plot here, but small glimpses of this shift in focus give the film its only real highlight. Particularly surprising is the middle act, which stays a lot further away from all the 'spook and boo' than is usual and instead focuses the lens on the main character as his sense of reality and sanity is brought into question not only by other characters (this is routine for a horror) but by the film itself. At one point I actually got thinking that maybe a Fight Club was going to get pulled, and Sutherland would be made out to really be insane, yet, sadly this isn't the case. Instead, going from slightly interesting to downright moronic, the film spirals into a netherworld of plot twists, contrived action involving a demon and then some more plot twists. To say that this closing act ruined the feature wouldn't be accurate; it was already ruined. Yet, if the film had ended where it should have I could have walked out with at least some respect for all involved. Alas, I cannot. Remember the ending to the latest Exorcist movie? Yeah, it's a little like that.Of course, I'm trying my best not to divulge into spoiler material, but then again I really don't recommend you see this anyway; even if you want to. Sure, if you enjoy such films as One Missed Call and Eye, then Mirrors is right up your alley, and well, you should never read any of my reviews again. However, if like me, you cannot stand these pseudo-thriller-mystery-horror movies that fail for the most part to scare on any level outside of contrived 'BOO' moments, then you should do as I advise and give this one a miss. With certain potential, particularly in Sutherland's character, and maybe less attempts at trying to blend the illogical with the logical (such as these movies often do) within a plot that really shouldn't be taken as seriously as it is here, Mirrors could have been a throwaway treat for horror fans, but instead ends up being something that should just be straight-out ignored. Mirrors can be genuine skin-crawl material, yet these moments are too few and far between to sustain any interest or momentum in amongst all the less successful moments.- A review by Jamie Robert Ward (http://www.invocus.net)</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>TheUnknown837-1</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>an offensively dull excuse for horror that defies intelligence and rationale and even has a major flaw for a horror movie: it's not scary</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Perhaps the reason why I despised Alexandre Aja's Mirrors is partially due to the fact that I am not a fan of the contemporary formula horror films that are all blood and guts and no story. All style and no substance. Why movies like this, and the Saw movies are so popular these days, I'll never know. But Mirrors is an offensively disturbing and dull movie that defies intelligence and rationale and it even has a major flaw for a horror movie: it's not scary.Aja's film, like One Missed Call (2008) is an American remake of an Asian horror film. In this case, Mirrors is a remake of the 2003 Korean film Into the Mirror, which was directed by Sung-ho Kim. I have not seen the said foreign film, but looking at its American remake, I think that it's basic plot was copied and any plot and effective story was left out. Mirrors has a creepy, but over-the-top plot. There is an evil force behind the world of mirrors (yes, there's a supernatural world behind the reflective glass) that wants revenge (or something) on a woman who was once a psychopathic killer. And as it turns out, anybody who becomes a night watchman and only a night watchman in the building where she was held for treatment, becomes a victim of the mirrors. This time the role of the sorry watchman goes to Kiefer Sutherland, who gives a performance as good as he as an actor could have done given the poor quality of the material he was given to work with. And as people die (in very gruesome, but far from scary manners), he begins to figure it out and goes on his own personal mission to solve the mystery before his family falls prey to the mirrors.Wow, what a plot! You got to be kidding me. Anyway, like I said, Mirrors is not a very scary film. It's a good-looking movie with some very good performances. Credit director Alexandre Aja for that, but discredit him and the screenwriters for the dull, ponderous story and the lack of chills and thrills or even general entertainment. Sometimes its disturbing, but in an offensive way. I will admit there was one scene, one of the gruesome killing scenes, that I felt my spine tingling and strayed a little bit before closing my eyes at night, but only for a few seconds. The rest of the horror scenes are cheap, over-the-top and recklessly bloody. Apparently, Aja did not study the Alfred Hitchcock rules of horror and suspense that make truly intense and scary films. But even still, Mirrors might have been better if there was a real plot and story and real characters behind it rather than just poor excuses for scares.What's more, the characters in the film, while well-acted, as offensively stupid in terms of their actions. For example, the Kiefer Sutherland character is a night watchman. He's suppose to keep intruders out of this building. So on his first night on the job, what does he do? He leaves the door wide open and walks away from it. What does he do when he finds a basement flooded with water? He wades waist-deep into it and turns on the electricity! And that's just two examples of countless examples of errors in intelligence and I will admit it that at these moments, I was actually mildly enjoying the film because my father and I were riffing on it like we were Joel and the robots on Mystery Science Theater 3000. That's really the only way you can enjoy a movie this bad.At two hours in length, Mirrors is far too long. At times, it's too convoluted and at other times, it's too simple. With a predictable storyline and a lack of scary moments, it proves to be nothing more than just a depressing time at the movies.</fullReview>
	</review>

When Carson sees his own reflection being tortured, he suffers the physical effects of his fractured visions. Suddenly the troubled ex-cop finds himself battling his personal demons and the ones that have hijacked his reflection, tormenting him with convulsions, spontaneous bleeding and near suffocation.His sympathetic but skeptical sister Angela (Amy Smart) dismisses these bizarre "nightmares" as a consequence of his stress and guilt over the accidental shooting, but Carson's estranged wife Amy (Paula Patton), a no-nonsense NYPD medical examiner, is less forgiving. Her husband's increasingly erratic behavior frightens her, pushing his family farther away?and, she fears, it's putting their children in danger.But a much deadlier threat looms, trapped within the mirrors and reflective surfaces that pervade their everyday life. As Carson investigates the mysterious disappearance of a Mayflower security guard and its possible connection to his ghastly visions, he realizes that a malevolent, otherworldly force is using reflections as a gateway to terrorize him and his family.If he has any hope of saving his wife and children from a horrifying death, Carson must somehow uncover the truth behind the mirrors?and convince Amy to help him battle the greatest evil he has ever faced. I usually try and forget movies like this, because I would have had a better time sitting in a theater with a soap opera playing.</fullReview>

	<review>
		<date>2008-08-15</date>
		<username>Guillermo Lande</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>Mirrors Is Feel Good Funny</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>My girlfriend and I just came from seeing Mirrors, and neither of us have laughed so hard and so much since we saw the Disney movie "Enchanted". My girlfriend actually had to watch part of the movie from the hallway so that all the people around us who took the movie so seriously wouldn't be offended by how hard the two of us were laughing. She was in tears, and I was in awe.The two of us debated on the way home whether the director was trying to be scary or trying to be funny. She guessed funny, and I thought part of the awesome humor was how seriously the movie took itself.It literally had scenes where the "monsters" were posing in front of the screen effectively saying, "I'm scaring you! I'm scaring you!" instead of attacking.It is, quite literally, beautifully ridiculous. To borrow a term from the director of "Batman the Movie", the movie Mirrors is theater of the absurd. It couldn't be more silly or more enjoyable if Keifer Sutherland had made a crack about a chimpanzee with a machine gun.This is a complete failure as a horror movie; but that's okay, because it's fantastically funny to watch and is wonderful entertainment. Both of us were delighted to have seen such a feel good horror movie.At no time ever, not in the dialog, in the setup, in the murders and spooks is this movie ever scary or even cohesive. It's like a board room of twelve year olds who didn't even speak English saw The Exorcist, The Ring, 1408, Dark Water, Lethal Weapon and every single Sci Fi Channel movie that revolves around the divorced couple and mashed random effects, cliché dialogs, reactions and spooks. It's so incohesive that frankly my girlfriend and I are shocked that so many people think this is a serious horror movie.Keep in mind, of the whole theater we suspect maybe only six people in the room laughed and realized this was silly and fun. Everybody else was biting their nails and talking to Keifer Sutherland trying to tell him what not to do so he wouldn't take wrong turns.If you love horror movies and love horror movie spoofs, forget all the movies that intentionally try to parody horror movies, and see this one that doesn't even realize it's the modern day Attack of the Killer Tomatoes.The reason I give this an eight is that I reserve the highest reviews for true quality movies like The Fall, El Orfanato, Apocalypto, etc. But we had so much fun watching this movie that I can't honestly give it less than an eight.</fullReview>
	</review>
